## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: chemical kinetics
**Analysis**: The target dA_dt is the instantaneous reaction rate (rate of change of concentration A). In chemical kinetics, the rate law typically expresses dA/dt as a function of reactant concentration A and, in non-isothermal or decaying-catalyst scenarios, explicit time t. This task seeks a symbolic model linking rate to A and t, consistent with various reaction orders, autocatalytic behaviors, time-dependent rate constants, or complex inhibition mechanisms.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Zero-order kinetics: dA_dt = c0
- First-order kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A
- Second-order kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A**2
- n-th order power law: dA_dt = c0 * A**c1
- Linear reversible/mixed-order: dA_dt = c0 + c1 * A
- Time-exponential rate constant: dA_dt = c0 * exp(c1 * t) * A
- Time-power rate constant: dA_dt = c0 * (t + c1)**c2 * A
- Autocatalytic kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A * (c1 - A)
- Substrate inhibition rational: dA_dt = c0 * A / (1 + c1 * A + c2 * A**2)
- Exponential saturation: dA_dt = c0 * (1 - exp(c1 * A))
- Sigmoidal activation (tanh): dA_dt = c0 * tanh(c1 * A + c2)
- Power-exponential coupling: dA_dt = c0 * A**c1 * exp(c2 * t)
- Biexponential time dependence: dA_dt = c0 * exp(c1 * t) + c2 * exp(c3 * t) * A
- Logarithmic time drift: dA_dt = c0 * log(t + c1) * A
- Threshold-linear activation: dA_dt = c0 * max(0, A - c1)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Rate independent of concentration A → zero-order kinetics (constant rate).
- Rate linearly proportional to A → first-order reaction (exponential decay of A).
- Rate proportional to A² → second-order (bimolecular) reaction.
- Rate with explicit time factor (e.g., multiplied by exp(c·t)) indicates time-varying temperature or catalyst deactivation.
- Rate vanishing at both low and high A, with a maximum in between, suggests autocatalytic (logistic‑like) kinetics → dA/dt ∝ A·(c − A).
- Rate first increasing then decreasing with A (substrate/excess inhibition) → rational function with A in numerator and A² in denominator.
- Non‑integer power exponent on A (fractional order) points to complex or heterogeneous reaction mechanisms.
- Sigmoidal dependence of rate on A (e.g., tanh, arctan) indicates cooperative or multi‑step activation effects.
